      <p>
       <b>
        Old French
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="fro" xml:lang="fro">
         franceis
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="fro" xml:lang="fro">
         françois
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <span lang="fro" xml:lang="fro">
         romanz
        </span>
       </i>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        Modern French
       </a>
       <i>
        <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr">
         <i>
          ancien français
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       ) was the language spoken in Northern France from the 8th century to the 14th century. In the 14th century, these dialects came to be collectively known as the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langue d'oïl">
         langue d'oïl
        </a>
       </i>
       , contrasting with the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27oc" title="Langue d'oc">
         langue d'oc
        </a>
       </i>
       or Occitan language in the south of France. The mid-14th century is taken as the transitional period to
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
        Middle French
       </a>
       , the language of the
       <a href="/wiki/French_Renaissance" title="French Renaissance">
        French Renaissance
       </a>
       , specifically based on the dialect of the
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Île-de-France">
        Île-de-France
       </a>
       region.
      </p>
      <p>
       The place and area where Old French was spoken natively roughly extended to the historical
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">
        Kingdom of France
       </a>
       and its vassals (including parts of the
       <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">
        Angevin Empire
       </a>
       , which during the 12th century remained under
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Normans" title="Anglo-Normans">
        Anglo-Norman
       </a>
       rule), and
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Burgundy" title="Duchy of Burgundy">
        Burgundy
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorraine_(duchy)" title="Lorraine (duchy)">
        Lorraine
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/County_of_Savoy" title="County of Savoy">
        Savoy
       </a>
       to the east (corresponding to modern north-central
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       , Belgian
       <a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">
        Wallonia
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Switzerland" title="Western Switzerland">
        western Switzerland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Piedmont" title="Piedmont">
        northwestern Italy
       </a>
       ), but the influence of Old French was much wider, as it was carried to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_England" title="Norman England">
        England
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">
        Sicily
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">
        Crusader states
       </a>
       as the language of a feudal elite and of commerce.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinoshita20063_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinoshita20063-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          </li>

        </li>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Areal_and_dialectal_divisions">
        Areal and dialectal divisions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Areal and dialectal divisions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langue d'oïl">
        Langue d'oïl
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance" title="Gallo-Romance">
        Gallo-Romance
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_France_1180-fr.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1124" data-file-width="988" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Map_France_1180-fr.svg/300px-Map_France_1180-fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Map_France_1180-fr.svg/450px-Map_France_1180-fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Map_France_1180-fr.svg/600px-Map_France_1180-fr.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_France_1180-fr.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map of France in 1180, at the height of the
         <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">
          feudal system
         </a>
         . The
         <a href="/wiki/Crown_lands_of_France" title="Crown lands of France">
          possessions of the French king
         </a>
         are in light blue, vassals to the French king in green,
         <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">
          Angevin
         </a>
         possessions in red. Shown in white is the
         <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">
          Holy Roman Empire
         </a>
         to the east, the western fringes of which, including
         <a href="/wiki/Upper_Burgundy" title="Upper Burgundy">
          Upper Burgundy
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Lorraine" title="Duchy of Lorraine">
          Lorraine
         </a>
         , were also part of the Old French areal.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Areal_linguistics" title="Areal linguistics">
        areal
       </a>
       of Old French in contemporary terms corresponded to the northern parts of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_France" title="Medieval France">
        Kingdom of France
       </a>
       (including
       <a href="/wiki/Anjou" title="Anjou">
        Anjou
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Normandy" title="Duchy of Normandy">
        Normandy
       </a>
       , which in the 12th century were ruled by the
       <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">
        Plantagenet kings of England
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_Burgundy" title="Upper Burgundy">
        Upper Burgundy
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Lorraine" title="Duchy of Lorraine">
        duchy of Lorraine
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">
        Norman dialect
       </a>
       was also spread to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_England" title="Norman England">
        England
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_Ireland" title="Norman Ireland">
        Ireland
       </a>
       , and during the
       <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
        crusades
       </a>
       , Old French was also spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">
        Kingdom of Sicily
       </a>
       , and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Antioch" title="Principality of Antioch">
        Principality of Antioch
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">
        Kingdom of Jerusalem
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Outremer" title="Outremer">
        Levant
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As part of the emerging
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance_languages" title="Gallo-Romance languages">
        Gallo-Romance
       </a>
       dialect continuum, the
       <i>
        langues d'oïl
       </i>
       were contrasted with the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
         langue d'oc
        </a>
       </i>
       (the emerging
       <a href="/wiki/Occitano-Romance_languages" title="Occitano-Romance languages">
        Occitano-Romance
       </a>
       group, at the time also called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proven%C3%A7al_(dialect)" title="Provençal (dialect)">
        Provençal
       </a>
       ), adjacent to the Old French area in the south-west, and with the
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo-Italic_languages" title="Gallo-Italic languages">
        Gallo-Italic
       </a>
       group to the south-east. The
       <a href="/wiki/Franco-Proven%C3%A7al_language" title="Franco-Provençal language">
        Franco-Provençal
       </a>
       group developed in Upper Burgundy, sharing features with both French and Provençal; it may have begun to diverge from the
       <i>
        langue d'oïl
       </i>
       as early as the 9th century, and is attested as a distinct Gallo-Romance variety by the 12th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       Dialects or variants of Old French included:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Burgundian_language_(O%C3%AFl)" title="Burgundian language (Oïl)">
         Burgundian
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Burgundy" title="Duchy of Burgundy">
         Burgundy
        </a>
        , then an independent
        <a href="/wiki/Duchy" title="Duchy">
         duchy
        </a>
        whose capital was at
        <a href="/wiki/Dijon" title="Dijon">
         Dijon
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Picard_language" title="Picard language">
         Picard
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Picardy" title="Picardy">
         Picardy
        </a>
        , whose principal cities were
        <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">
         Calais
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Lille" title="Lille">
         Lille
        </a>
        . It was said that the Picard language began at the east door of
        <a href="/wiki/Notre-Dame_de_Paris" title="Notre-Dame de Paris">
         Notre-Dame de Paris
        </a>
        , so far-reaching was its influence;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Norman" title="Old Norman">
         Old Norman
        </a>
        , in
        <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">
         Normandy
        </a>
        , whose principal cities were
        <a href="/wiki/Caen" title="Caen">
         Caen
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">
         Rouen
        </a>
        . The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest" title="Norman conquest">
         Norman conquest
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
         England
        </a>
        brought many Norman-speaking aristocrats into the British Isles. Most of the older Norman (sometimes called "French") words in
        <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
         English
        </a>
        reflect its influence, which became a conduit for the introduction into the Anglo-Norman realm, as did Anglo-Norman control of Anjou and Gascony and other continental possessions.
        <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">
         Anglo-Norman
        </a>
        was a language that reflected a shared culture on both sides of the
        <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">
         English Channel
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lusignan_3-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Lusignan-3">
          [3]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Ultimately, the language declined and fell, becoming
        <a href="/wiki/Law_French" title="Law French">
         Law French
        </a>
        , a jargon spoken by lawyers that was used in
        <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">
         English law
        </a>
        until the reign of
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">
         Charles II of England
        </a>
        .
        <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
         Norman
        </a>
        , however, still survives in Normandy and the
        <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
         Channel Islands
        </a>
        , as a regional language;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Walloon_language" title="Walloon language">
         Wallon
        </a>
        , around
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namur_(city)" title="Namur (city)">
         Namur
        </a>
        , now in
        <a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">
         Wallonia
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
         Belgium
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Gallo_language" title="Gallo language">
         Gallo
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brittany" title="Duchy of Brittany">
         Duchy of Brittany
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Lorrain_language" title="Lorrain language">
         Lorrain
        </a>
        of the
        <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Lorraine" title="Duchy of Lorraine">
         Duchy of Lorraine
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lenguas_galorromance.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="694" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Lenguas_galorromance.png/220px-Lenguas_galorromance.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Lenguas_galorromance.png/330px-Lenguas_galorromance.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Lenguas_galorromance.png/440px-Lenguas_galorromance.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lenguas_galorromance.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Distribution of the modern
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langue d'oïl">
           langue d'oïl
          </a>
         </i>
         (shades of green) and of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco-Proven%C3%A7al" title="Franco-Provençal">
          Franco-Provençal
         </a>
         dialects (shades of blue)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Some modern languages are derived from Old French dialects other than Classical French, which is based on the
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Île-de-France">
        Île-de-France
       </a>
       dialect. They include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angevin_(language)" title="Angevin (language)">
        Angevin
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berrichon" title="Berrichon">
        Berrichon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Burgundian_language_(O%C3%AFl)" title="Burgundian language (Oïl)">
        Bourguignon-Morvandiau
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Champenois" title="Champenois">
        Champenois
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Frainc-Comtou_dialect" title="Frainc-Comtou dialect">
        Franc-Comtois
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gallo_language" title="Gallo language">
        Gallo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lorrain_language" title="Lorrain language">
        Lorrain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">
        Norman
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Picard_language" title="Picard language">
        Picard
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poitevin_language" title="Poitevin language">
        Poitevin
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saintongeais" title="Saintongeais">
        Saintongeais
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Walloon_language" title="Walloon language">
        Walloon
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_from_Vulgar_Latin">
        Evolution from Vulgar Latin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolution from Vulgar Latin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Beginning with
       <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">
        Plautus
       </a>
       ’s time (254–184
       <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">
        b.c.
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">
        Classical Latin
       </a>
       ’s phonological structure changed, eventually yielding
       <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
        Vulgar Latin
       </a>
       , the common spoken language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">
        Western Roman Empire
       </a>
       . This latter form differed strongly from its classical counterpart in phonology and morphology, as well as exhibiting differences in lexicon; it was the ancestor of the
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance languages
       </a>
       , including Old French.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Latin_influences">
        Non-Latin influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Non-Latin influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gaulish">
        Gaulish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Gaulish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_French_words_of_Gaulish_origin" title="List of French words of Gaulish origin">
        List of French words of Gaulish origin
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Some
       <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">
        Gaulish
       </a>
       words influenced Vulgar Latin and, through this, other Romance languages. For example, classical Latin
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/equus" title="wikt:equus">
         equus
        </a>
       </i>
       was uniformly replaced in Vulgar Latin by
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caballus" title="wikt:caballus">
         caballus
        </a>
       </i>
       ‘nag, work horse’, derived from Gaulish
       <i>
        caballos
       </i>
       (cf.
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh
       </a>
       <i>
        <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
         ceffyl
        </span>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       <i>
        <span lang="br" xml:lang="br">
         kefel
        </span>
       </i>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       giving Modern French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cheval" title="wikt:cheval">
         cheval
        </a>
       </i>
       , Occitan
       <i>
        caval
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        chaval
       </i>
       ), Catalan
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cavall" title="wikt:cavall">
         cavall
        </a>
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caballo" title="wikt:caballo">
         caballo
        </a>
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cavalo" title="wikt:cavalo">
         cavalo
        </a>
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cavallo" title="wikt:cavallo">
         cavallo
        </a>
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cal" title="wikt:cal">
         cal
        </a>
       </i>
       , and, by extension, English
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cavalry" title="wikt:cavalry">
         cavalry
        </a>
       </i>
       . An estimated 200 words of Gaulish etymology survive in modern French, for example
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ch%C3%AAne" title="wikt:chêne">
        <i>
         chêne
        </i>
       </a>
       ‘oak tree’ and
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/charrue" title="wikt:charrue">
        <i>
         charrue
        </i>
       </a>
       ‘plough’.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite attempts to explain some phonetic changes being caused by a Gaulish substrate, only one of them is certain, because this fact is clearly attested in the Gaulish-language
       <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">
        epigraphy
       </a>
       on the pottery found at
       <a href="/wiki/La_Graufesenque" title="La Graufesenque">
        la Graufesenque
       </a>
       (
       <small>
        A.D.
       </small>
       1st century). There, the Greek word
       <i>
        paropsid-es
       </i>
       (written in Latin) appears as
       <i>
        paraxsid-i
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The consonant clusters /ps/ and /pt/ shifted to /xs/ and /xt/, e.g. Latin
       <i>
        capsa
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        *kaxsa
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        caisse
       </i>
       (
       <abbr title="unlike">
        ≠
       </abbr>
       Italian
       <i>
        cassa
       </i>
       ) or
       <i>
        captīvus
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        *kaxtivus
       </i>
       &gt; OF
       <i>
        chaitif
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (mod.
       <i>
        chétif
       </i>
       ; cf. Irish
       <i>
        cacht
       </i>
       ‘servant’; ≠ Italian
       <i>
        cattiv-ità
       </i>
       , Portuguese "cativo", Spanish
       <i>
        cautivo
       </i>
       ). This phonetic evolution is parallel to the shift of the Latin cluster /kt/ in Old French (Latin
       <i>
        factum
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        fait
       </i>
       , ≠ Italian
       <i>
        fatto
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        feito
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        hecho
       </i>
       ; or
       <i>
        lactem
       </i>
       * &gt;
       <i>
        lait
       </i>
       , ≠ Italian
       <i>
        latte
       </i>
       , Portuguese
       <i>
        leite
       </i>
       , Spanish
       <i>
        leche
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Frankish">
        Frankish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Frankish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_French_words_of_Germanic_origin" title="List of French words of Germanic origin">
        List of French words of Germanic origin
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The pronunciation, vocabulary, and syntax of the Vulgar Latin spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Gaul" title="Roman Gaul">
        Roman Gaul
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">
        Late Antiquity
       </a>
       was modified by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Frankish_language" title="Old Frankish language">
        Old Frankish language
       </a>
       , spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">
        Franks
       </a>
       who settled in Gaul from the 5th century and conquered the entire Old French-speaking area by the 530s. The name
       <i>
        français
       </i>
       itself is derived from the name the Franks.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Old Frankish language had a definitive influence on the development of Old French, which partly explains why the earliest attested Old French documents are older than the earliest attestations in other Romance languages (e.g.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strasbourg_Oath" title="Strasbourg Oath">
        Strasbourg Oaths
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sequence_of_Saint_Eulalia" title="Sequence of Saint Eulalia">
        Sequence of Saint Eulalia
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is the result of an earlier gap created between Classical Latin and its evolved forms, which slowly reduced and eventually severed the intercomprehensibility between the two. The
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Dutch" title="Old Dutch">
        Old Low Franconian
       </a>
       influence is also believed to be responsible for the differences between the
       <i>
        langue d′oïl
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        <i>
         langue d′oc
        </i>
       </a>
       (Occitan), being that various parts of Northern France remained bilingual between Latin and Germanic for some time,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and these areas correspond precisely to where the first documents in Old French were written.
      </p>
      <p>
       This Germanic language shaped the popular Latin spoken here and gave it a very distinctive identity compared to the other future Romance languages. The very first noticeable influence is the substitution of the Latin melodic accent by a Germanic stress
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and its result was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diphthongization" title="Diphthongization">
        diphthongization
       </a>
       , differentiation between long and short vowels, the fall of the unaccented syllable and of the final vowels:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Latin
        <i>
         decimus
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         -a
        </i>
        ‘tenth’ &gt; OF
        <i>
         disme
        </i>
        &gt; F
        <i>
         dîme
        </i>
        ‘tenth’ (&gt; E
        <i>
         dime
        </i>
        ; Italian
        <i>
         decimo
        </i>
        , Portuguese
        <i>
         décimo
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         diezmo
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">
         VL
        </a>
        <i>
         dignitate
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         deintié
        </i>
        (&gt; E
        <i>
         dainty
        </i>
        ; Italian
        <i>
         dignità
        </i>
        , Romanian
        <i>
         demnitate
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        VL
        <i>
         catena
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         chaeine
        </i>
        (&gt; E
        <i>
         chain
        </i>
        ; Occitan, Portuguese
        <i>
         cadeia
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         cadena
        </i>
        , Italian
        <i>
         catena
        </i>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Additionally, two phonemes that had long since died out in Vulgar Latin were reintroduced:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [h]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [w]
       </span>
       (&gt; OF
       <i>
        g(u)-
       </i>
       , ONF
       <i>
        w-
       </i>
       cf.
       <a href="/wiki/Picard_language" title="Picard language">
        Picard
       </a>
       <i>
        w-
       </i>
       ):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        VL
        <i>
         altu
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         halt
        </i>
        ‘high’ (influenced by OLF
        <i>
         *hōh
        </i>
        ; ≠ Italian, Portuguese and Spanish
        <i>
         alto
        </i>
        , Occitan
        <i>
         naut
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        L
        <i>
         vespa
        </i>
        &gt; F
        <i>
         guêpe
        </i>
        , Picard
        <i>
         wèpe
        </i>
        , Wallon
        <i>
         wèsse
        </i>
        , all ‘wasp’ (influenced by OLF
        <i>
         *wapsa
        </i>
        ; ≠ Occitan
        <i>
         vèspa
        </i>
        , Italian and Portuguese
        <i>
         vespa
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         avispa
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        L
        <i>
         viscus
        </i>
        &gt; F
        <i>
         gui
        </i>
        ‘mistletoe’ (influenced by OLF
        <i>
         *wīhsila
        </i>
        ‘morello’ with analogous fruits, when they are not ripe; ≠ Occitan
        <i>
         vesc
        </i>
        , Italian
        <i>
         vischio
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        LL
        <i>
         vulpiculu
        </i>
        ‘fox kit’ (from L
        <i>
         vulpes
        </i>
        ‘fox’) &gt; OF
        <i>
         golpilz
        </i>
        , Picard
        <i>
         woupil
        </i>
        ‘fox’ (influenced by OLF
        <i>
         *wulf
        </i>
        ‘wolf’; ≠ Occitan
        <i>
         volpìlh
        </i>
        , Old Italian
        <i>
         volpiglio
        </i>
        , Spanish
        <i>
         vulpeja
        </i>
        ‘vixen’)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       On the opposite, the Italian, Portuguese and Spanish words of Germanic origin borrowed from French or directly from Germanic retain
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /gw/
       </span>
       ~
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /g/
       </span>
       , e.g. It, Sp.
       <i>
        guerra
       </i>
       ‘war’, alongside
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /g/
       </span>
       in French
       <i>
        guerre
       </i>
       ). In these examples, we notice a clear consequence of bilingualism, that sometimes even changed the first syllable of the Latin words. One example of a Latin word influencing an Old Low Franconian loan is
       <i>
        framboise
       </i>
       ‘raspberry’, from OF
       <i>
        frambeise
       </i>
       , from OLF
       <i>
        *brāmbesi
       </i>
       ‘blackberry’ (cf. Dutch
       <i>
        braambes
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        braambezie
       </i>
       ; akin to German
       <i>
        Brombeere
       </i>
       , English dial.
       <i>
        bramberry
       </i>
       ) blended with LL
       <i>
        fraga
       </i>
       or OF
       <i>
        fraie
       </i>
       ‘strawberry’, which explains the replacement
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [b]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [f]
       </span>
       and in turn the final
       <i>
        -se
       </i>
       of
       <i>
        framboise
       </i>
       added to OF
       <i>
        fraie
       </i>
       to make
       <i>
        freise
       </i>
       , modern
       <i>
        fraise
       </i>
       (≠ Wallon
       <i>
        frève
       </i>
       , Romanian
       <i>
        fragă
       </i>
       , Romansh
       <i>
        fraja
       </i>
       , Italian
       <i>
        fragola
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        fravola
       </i>
       ‘strawberry’).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Pope (1934) estimated that perhaps still 15% of the vocabulary of modern French derives from Germanic sources (while the proportion was larger in Old French, because the French language borrowed heavily from Latin and Italian).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_written_Old_French">
        Earliest written Old French
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Earliest written Old French">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       At the third
       <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Tours" title="Council of Tours">
        Council of Tours
       </a>
       in 813, priests were ordered to preach in the vernacular language (either Romance or Germanic), since the common people could no longer understand formal Latin.
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest documents said to be written in the Gallo-Romance that presages French – after the
       <a href="/wiki/Reichenau_Glosses" title="Reichenau Glosses">
        Reichenau
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kassel_glosses" title="Kassel glosses">
        Kassel glosses
       </a>
       (8th and 9th centuries) – are the
       <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">
        Oaths of Strasbourg
       </a>
       (treaties and charters into which King
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">
        Charles the Bald
       </a>
       entered in 842):
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        Pro Deo amur et pro Christian poblo et nostro commun salvament, d’ist di en avant, in quant Deus savir et podir me dunat, si salvarai eo cist meon fradre Karlo, et in aiudha et in cadhuna cosa...
       </p>
       <p>
        (For the love of God and for the Christian people, and our common salvation, from this day forward, as God will give me the knowledge and the power, I will defend my brother Charles with my help in everything...)
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       The second-oldest document in Old French is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eulalia_sequence" title="Eulalia sequence">
        Eulalia sequence
       </a>
       , which is important for linguistic reconstruction of Old French pronunciation due to its consistent spelling.
      </p>
      <p>
       The royal
       <a href="/wiki/House_of_Capet" title="House of Capet">
        House of Capet
       </a>
       , founded by
       <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Capet" title="Hugh Capet">
        Hugh Capet
       </a>
       in 987, inaugurated the development of northern French culture in and around
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Île-de-France">
        Île-de-France
       </a>
       , which slowly but firmly asserted its ascendency over the more southerly areas of
       <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">
        Aquitaine
       </a>
       and Tolosa (
       <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">
        Toulouse
       </a>
       ). The
       <a href="/wiki/Capetian_dynasty" title="Capetian dynasty">
        Capetians
       </a>
       '
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langue d'oïl">
         langue d'oïl
        </a>
       </i>
       , the forerunner of modern standard French, did not begin to become the common speech of all of France, however, until after the
       <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">
        French Revolution
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_Middle_French">
        Transition to Middle French
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Transition to Middle French">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
        Middle French
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the Late Middle Ages, the Old French dialects diverged into a number of distinct
       <i>
        langues d'oïl
       </i>
       , among which
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
        Middle French
       </a>
       proper was the dialect of the
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France" title="Île-de-France">
        Île-de-France
       </a>
       region. During the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_France" title="Early modern France">
        Early Modern period
       </a>
       , French now becomes established as the official language of the Kingdom of France throughout the realm, also including the
       <i>
        langue d'oc
       </i>
       -speaking territories in the south. It was only in the 17th to 18th centuries – with the development especially of popular literature of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_bleue" title="Bibliothèque bleue">
         Bibliothèque bleue
        </a>
       </i>
       – that a standardized
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_French" title="Classical French">
        Classical French
       </a>
       spread throughout France alongside the regional dialects.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Medieval_French_literature" title="Medieval French literature">
        Medieval French literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The material and cultural conditions in
       <a href="/wiki/Territorial_evolution_of_France" title="Territorial evolution of France">
        France and associated territories
       </a>
       around the year 1100 triggered what
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Homer_Haskins" title="Charles Homer Haskins">
        Charles Homer Haskins
       </a>
       termed the "
       <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">
        Renaissance of the 12th century
       </a>
       ", resulting in a profusion of creative works in a variety of genres. Old French gives way to
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
        Middle French
       </a>
       in the mid-14th century, paving the way for early
       <a href="/wiki/French_Renaissance_literature" title="French Renaissance literature">
        French Renaissance literature
       </a>
       of the 15th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest extant French literary texts date from the ninth century, but very few texts before the 11th century have survived. The first literary works written in Old French were
       <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">
        saints' lives
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sequence_of_Saint_Eulalia" title="Sequence of Saint Eulalia">
         Canticle of Saint Eulalie
        </a>
       </i>
       , written in the second half of the 9th century, is generally accepted as the first such text.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the beginning of the 13th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Bodel" title="Jean Bodel">
        Jean Bodel
       </a>
       , in his
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chanson_de_Saisnes" title="Chanson de Saisnes">
         Chanson de Saisnes
        </a>
       </i>
       , divided medieval French narrative literature into three subject areas: the
       <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_France" title="Matter of France">
        Matter of France
       </a>
       or Matter of
       <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
        Charlemagne
       </a>
       ; the
       <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Rome" title="Matter of Rome">
        Matter of Rome
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" title="Romance (heroic literature)">
        romances
       </a>
       in an ancient setting); and the
       <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">
        Matter of Britain
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arthurian_romance" title="Arthurian romance">
        Arthurian romances
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_lai" title="Breton lai">
        Breton lais
       </a>
       ). The first of these is the subject area of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chanson_de_geste" title="Chanson de geste">
         chansons de geste
        </a>
       </i>
       ("songs of exploits" or "songs of (heroic) deeds"),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epic_poem" title="Epic poem">
        epic poems
       </a>
       typically composed in
       <a href="/wiki/Decasyllable" title="Decasyllable">
        ten-syllable
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Assonance" title="Assonance">
        assonanced
       </a>
       (occasionally
       <a href="/wiki/Rhyme" title="Rhyme">
        rhymed
       </a>
       )
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Laisse" title="Laisse">
         laisses
        </a>
       </i>
       . More than one hundred
       <i>
        chansons de geste
       </i>
       have survived in around three hundred manuscripts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The oldest and most celebrated of the
       <i>
        chansons de geste
       </i>
       is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_Roland" title="The Song of Roland">
         The Song of Roland
        </a>
       </i>
       (earliest version composed in the late 11th century).
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_de_Bar-sur-Aube" title="Bertrand de Bar-sur-Aube">
        Bertrand de Bar-sur-Aube
       </a>
       in his
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Girart_de_Vienne" title="Girart de Vienne">
         Girart de Vienne
        </a>
       </i>
       set out a grouping of the
       <i>
        chansons de geste
       </i>
       into three
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literature_cycle" title="Literature cycle">
        cycles
       </a>
       : the
       <i>
        Geste du roi
       </i>
       centering on Charlemagne, the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geste_de_Garin_de_Monglane" title="Geste de Garin de Monglane">
         Geste de Garin de Monglane
        </a>
       </i>
       (whose central character was
       <a href="/wiki/William_of_Gellone" title="William of Gellone">
        William of Orange
       </a>
       ), and the
       <i>
        Geste de
        <a href="/wiki/Doon_de_Mayence" title="Doon de Mayence">
         Doon de Mayence
        </a>
       </i>
       or the "rebel vassal cycle", the most famous characters of which were
       <a href="/wiki/Renaud_de_Montauban" title="Renaud de Montauban">
        Renaud de Montauban
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Girart_de_Roussillon" title="Girart de Roussillon">
        Girart de Roussillon
       </a>
       . A fourth grouping, not listed by Bertrand, is the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Crusade_cycle" title="Crusade cycle">
         Crusade cycle
        </a>
       </i>
       , dealing with the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">
        First Crusade
       </a>
       and its immediate aftermath.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Jean_Bodel" title="Jean Bodel">
        Jean Bodel
       </a>
       's other two categories—the "Matter of Rome" and the "Matter of Britain"—concern the French
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_(heroic_literature)" title="Romance (heroic literature)">
        romance
       </a>
       or
       <i>
        roman
       </i>
       . Around a hundred verse romances survive from the period 1150–1220.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From around 1200 on, the tendency was increasingly to write the romances in prose (many of the earlier verse romances were adapted into prose versions), although new verse romances continued to be written to the end of the 14th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most important romance of the 13th century is the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Rose" title="Romance of the Rose">
         Romance of the Rose
        </a>
       </i>
       which breaks considerably from the conventions of the chivalric adventure story.
      </p>
      <p>
       Medieval French
       <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">
        lyric poetry
       </a>
       was indebted to the poetic and cultural traditions in
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">
        Southern France
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Provence" title="Provence">
        Provence
       </a>
       —including
       <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">
        Toulouse
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Poitiers" title="Poitiers">
        Poitiers
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">
        Aquitaine
       </a>
       region—where
       <i>
        langue d'oc
       </i>
       was spoken (
       <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">
        Occitan language
       </a>
       ); in their turn, the Provençal poets were greatly influenced by poetic traditions from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_Spain" title="Muslim Spain">
        Hispano-Arab world
       </a>
       . The Occitan or Provençal poets were called
       <a href="/wiki/Troubadour" title="Troubadour">
        troubadours
       </a>
       , from the word
       <i>
        trobar
       </i>
       "to find, to invent". Lyric poets in Old French are called
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trouv%C3%A8res" title="Trouvères">
         trouvères
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       By the late 13th century, the poetic tradition in France had begun to develop in ways that differed significantly from the troubadour poets, both in content and in the use of certain fixed forms. The new poetic (as well as musical: some of the earliest medieval music has lyrics composed in Old French by the earliest composers known by name) tendencies are apparent in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Roman_de_Fauvel" title="Roman de Fauvel">
         Roman de Fauvel
        </a>
       </i>
       in 1310 and 1314, a satire on abuses in the medieval church, filled with medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Motet" title="Motet">
        motets
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lai_(poetic_form)" title="Lai (poetic form)">
        lais
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rondeau_(music)" title="Rondeau (music)">
        rondeaux
       </a>
       and other new secular forms of poetry and music (mostly anonymous, but with several pieces by
       <a href="/wiki/Philippe_de_Vitry" title="Philippe de Vitry">
        Philippe de Vitry
       </a>
       , who would coin the expression
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ars_nova" title="Ars nova">
         ars nova
        </a>
       </i>
       to distinguish the new musical practice from the music of the immediately preceding age). The best-known poet and composer of
       <i>
        ars nova
       </i>
       secular music and chansons of the incipient
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
        Middle French
       </a>
       period was
       <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_de_Machaut" title="Guillaume de Machaut">
        Guillaume de Machaut
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Discussions about the origins of non-religious theater (
       <i>
        théâtre profane
       </i>
       ) – both drama and farce—in the Middle Ages remain controversial, but the idea of a continuous popular tradition stemming from Latin comedy and tragedy to the 9th century seems unlikely. Most historians place the origin of medieval
       <i>
        drama
       </i>
       in the church's liturgical dialogues and "tropes".
       <a href="/wiki/Mystery_play" title="Mystery play">
        Mystery plays
       </a>
       were eventually transferred from the monastery church to the chapter house or refectory hall and finally to the open air, and the vernacular was substituted for Latin. In the 12th century one finds the earliest extant passages in French appearing as refrains inserted into
       <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_drama" title="Liturgical drama">
        liturgical dramas
       </a>
       in Latin, such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Nicholas" title="Saint Nicholas">
        Saint Nicholas
       </a>
       (patron saint of the student clercs) play and a
       <a href="/wiki/Saint_Stephen" title="Saint Stephen">
        Saint Stephen
       </a>
       play. An early French dramatic play is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Le_Jeu_d%27Adam" title="Le Jeu d'Adam">
         Le Jeu d'Adam
        </a>
       </i>
       (c. 1150) written in octosyllabic rhymed couplets with Latin stage directions (implying that it was written by Latin-speaking clerics for a lay public).
      </p>
      <p>
       A large body of
       <a href="/wiki/Fable" title="Fable">
        fables
       </a>
       survive in Old French; these include (mostly anonymous) literature dealing with the recurring trickster character of
       <a href="/wiki/Reynard" title="Reynard">
        Reynard
       </a>
       the
       <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">
        Fox
       </a>
       . Marie de France was also active in this genre, producing the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ysopet" title="Ysopet">
         Ysopet
        </a>
       </i>
       (Little
       <a href="/wiki/Aesop" title="Aesop">
        Aesop
       </a>
       ) series of fables in verse. Related to the fable was the more bawdy
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fabliau" title="Fabliau">
         fabliau
        </a>
       </i>
       , which covered topics such as cuckolding and corrupt clergy. These
       <i>
        fabliaux
       </i>
       would be an important source for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaucer" title="Chaucer">
        Chaucer
       </a>
       and for the Renaissance short story (
       <i>
        conte
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        nouvelle
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_French" title="Phonological history of French">
        Phonological history of French
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Old French was constantly changing and evolving. However, the form in the late 12th century, as attested in a great deal of mostly poetic writings, can be considered standard. The writing system at this time was more phonetic than that used in most subsequent centuries. In particular, all written consonants (including final ones) were pronounced, except for
       <i>
        s
       </i>
       preceding non-
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stop consonants
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       in
       <i>
        et
       </i>
       , and final
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       was pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel#Mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">
         ə
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       . The phonological system can be summarised as follows:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <p>
       Notes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        All
        <a href="/wiki/Obstruent" title="Obstruent">
         obstruents
        </a>
        (plosives, fricatives and affricates) were subject to
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Word-final_devoicing" title="Word-final devoicing">
         word-final devoicing
        </a>
        , which was usually indicated in the orthography.
       </li>
       <li>
        The
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Affricate" title="Affricate">
         affricates
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ts/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dz/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʃ/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /dʒ/
        </span>
        became
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">
         fricatives
        </a>
        (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [s]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [z]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʃ]
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ʒ]
        </span>
        ) in
        <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
         Middle French
        </a>
        .
        <ul>
         <li>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ts/
          </span>
          had three spellings –
          <i>
           c
          </i>
          before
          <i>
           e
          </i>
          or
          <i>
           i
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           ç
          </i>
          before other vowels, or
          <i>
           z
          </i>
          at the end of a word – as seen in
          <i>
           <b>
            c
           </b>
           ent
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           chan
           <b>
            ç
           </b>
           on
          </i>
          ,
          <i>
           pri
           <b>
            z
           </b>
          </i>
          ("a hundred, song, price").
         </li>
         <li>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /dz/
          </span>
          was written as
          <i>
           z
          </i>
          , as in
          <i>
           do
           <b>
            z
           </b>
           e
          </i>
          "twelve", and did not occur word-initially.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʎ/
        </span>
        (
        <i>
         l
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mouill%C3%A9" title="Mouillé">
          mouillé
         </a>
        </i>
        ), as in
        <i>
         conse
         <b>
          il
         </b>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         trava
         <b>
          ill
         </b>
         ier
        </i>
        ("advice, to work"), became
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /j/
        </span>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
         Modern French
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɲ/
        </span>
        appeared not only in the middle of a word, but also at the end, as in
        <i>
         poi
         <b>
          ng
         </b>
        </i>
        "fist". At the end of a word,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɲ/
        </span>
        was later lost, leaving a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasalized_vowel" title="Nasalized vowel">
         nasalized vowel
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        was found only in Germanic loanwords and was later lost (although it is
        <a href="/wiki/Cheshirization" title="Cheshirization">
         cheshirized
        </a>
        as the so-called
        <a href="/wiki/Aspirated_h" title="Aspirated h">
         aspirated h
        </a>
        that blocks
        <a href="/wiki/Liaison_(French)" title="Liaison (French)">
         liaison
        </a>
        ). In native Latin words,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        was lost early on, as in
        <i>
         om
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         uem
        </i>
        , from Latin
        <i>
         homō
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Intervocalic
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /d/
        </span>
        from both Latin
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /t/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /d/
        </span>
        was
        <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
         lenited
        </a>
        to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ð]
        </span>
        in the early period (cf. contemporary Spanish:
        <i>
         amado
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [aˈmaðo]
        </span>
        ). At the end of words it was also devoiced to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [θ]
        </span>
        . In some texts it was sometimes written as
        <i>
         dh
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         th
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         aiudha, cadhuna, Ludher, vithe
        </i>
        ). By 1100 it disappeared altogether.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
         <a href="#cite_note-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In Old French, the nasal vowels were not separate phonemes but only
       <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">
        allophones
       </a>
       of the oral vowels before a nasal consonant. The nasal consonant was fully pronounced;
       <i>
        bon
       </i>
       was pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bõn]
       </span>
       (Modern French
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bɔ̃]
       </span>
       ). Nasal vowels were present even in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_syllable" title="Open syllable">
        open syllables
       </a>
       before nasals where Modern French has oral vowels, as in
       <i>
        bone
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bõnə]
       </span>
       (Modern French
       <i>
        bonne
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bɔn]
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Monophthongs">
        Monophthongs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Monophthongs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [ã]
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Notes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /o/
        </span>
        had formerly existed but closed to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /u/
        </span>
        ; the original Western Romance
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /u/
        </span>
        having previously been fronted to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /y/
        </span>
        across most of what is now France and northern Italy.
        <ul>
         <li>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /o/
          </span>
          would later appear again when
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /aw/
          </span>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophthongize" title="Monophthongize">
           monophthongized
          </a>
          and also when
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ɔ/
          </span>
          closed in certain positions (such as when it was followed by original
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /s/
          </span>
          or
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /z/
          </span>
          but not by
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ts/
          </span>
          , which later became
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /s/
          </span>
          ).
         </li>
         <li>
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /õ/
          </span>
          may have similarly been closed to
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ũ/
          </span>
          , in at least in some dialects, since it was borrowed into
          <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">
           Middle English
          </a>
          as
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /uːn/
          </span>
          &gt;
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /aʊn/
          </span>
          (Latin
          <i>
           computāre
          </i>
          &gt; OF
          <i>
           conter
          </i>
          &gt; English
          <i>
           count
          </i>
          ; Latin
          <i>
           rotundum
          </i>
          &gt; OF
          <i>
           ront
          </i>
          &gt; English
          <i>
           round
          </i>
          ; Latin
          <i>
           bonitātem
          </i>
          &gt; OF
          <i>
           bonté
          </i>
          &gt; English
          <i>
           bounty
          </i>
          ). In any case, traces of such a change were erased in later stages of French, when the close nasal vowels
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ĩ ỹ õ~ũ/
          </span>
          were opened to become
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /ɛ̃ œ̃ ɔ̃/
          </span>
          .
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ə̃/
        </span>
        may have existed in the unstressed third-person plural verb ending
        <i>
         -ent
        </i>
        , but it may have already passed to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ə/
        </span>
        , which is known to have happened by the Middle French period at the latest.
       </li>
      </ul>
