        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Dieu#Old_French" title="wikt:Dieu">
          Dieu
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         God
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /wew/
         </span>
         ~
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /wøw/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/jueu#Old_French" title="wikt:jueu">
          jueu
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         Jew
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Notes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        In Early Old French (up to about the mid-12th century), the spelling ⟨ai⟩ represented a diphthong
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aj/
        </span>
        instead of the later
        <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
         monophthong
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
         <a href="#cite_note-23">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and ⟨ei⟩ represented the diphthong
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ej/
        </span>
        , which merged with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /oj/
        </span>
        in Late Old French (except when it was nasalized).
       </li>
       <li>
        In Early Old French, the diphthongs described above as "rising" may have been falling diphthongs (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ie̯/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /yj/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ue̯/
        </span>
        ). In earlier works with vowel
        <a href="/wiki/Assonance" title="Assonance">
         assonance
        </a>
        , the diphthong written ⟨ie⟩ did not assonate with any pure vowels, which suggests that it cannot have simply been
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /je/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The pronunciation of the vowels written ⟨ue⟩ and ⟨eu⟩ is debated. In the first records of Early Old French, they represented and were written as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /uo/, /ou/
        </span>
        , and by
        <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">
         Middle French
        </a>
        , they had both merged as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ø ~ œ/
        </span>
        , but the transitional pronunciations are unclear.
       </li>
       <li>
        Early Old French had additional triphthongs
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iej/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /uoj/
        </span>
        (equivalent to diphthongs followed by
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /j/
        </span>
        ); these soon merged into
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɥi/
        </span>
        respectively.
       </li>
       <li>
        The diphthong ⟨iu⟩ was rare and had merged into ⟨ui⟩ by Middle French (OF
        <i>
         tiule
        </i>
        &gt; MF
        <i>
         tuile
        </i>
        'tile'; OF
        <i>
         siure
        </i>
        &gt; Late OF
        <i>
         suire
        </i>
        &gt; MF
        <i>
         suivre
        </i>
        'follow').
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hiatus">
        Hiatus
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hiatus">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In addition to diphthongs, Old French had many instances of
       <a href="/wiki/Hiatus_(linguistics)" title="Hiatus (linguistics)">
        hiatus
       </a>
       between adjacent vowels because of the loss of an intervening consonant. Manuscripts generally do not distinguish hiatus from true diphthongs, but modern scholarly transcription indicates it with a
       <a href="/wiki/Diaeresis_(diacritic)" title="Diaeresis (diacritic)">
        diaeresis
       </a>
       , as in Modern French:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Latin
        <i>
         audīre
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         oïr
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /oˈir/
        </span>
        'hear'
       </li>
       <li>
        Vulgar Latin *
        <i>
         vidūtum
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         veü
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /vəˈy/
        </span>
        'seen'
       </li>
       <li>
        Latin
        <i>
         rēgīnam
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         reïne
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /rəˈinə/
        </span>
        'queen'
       </li>
       <li>
        Latin
        <i>
         pāgēnsem
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         païs
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /paˈis/
        </span>
        'country'
       </li>
       <li>
        Latin
        <i>
         augustum
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         aoust
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aˈust/
        </span>
        'August'
       </li>
       <li>
        Latin
        <i>
         patellam
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         paele
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /paˈɛlə/
        </span>
        'pan'
       </li>
       <li>
        Late Latin
        <i>
         quaternum
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         quaïer
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kwaˈjer/
        </span>
        'booklet, quire'
       </li>
       <li>
        Late Latin
        <i>
         aetāticum
        </i>
        &gt; OF
        <i>
         aage
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         eage
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aˈad͡ʒə/ ~ /əˈad͡ʒə/
        </span>
        'age'
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">
        Nouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Old French maintained a two-case system, with a
       <a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">
        nominative case
       </a>
       and an
       <a href="/wiki/Oblique_case" title="Oblique case">
        oblique case
       </a>
       , for longer than did some other Romance languages like
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       . Case distinctions, at least in the masculine
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
        gender
       </a>
       , were marked on both the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definite_article" title="Definite article">
        definite article
       </a>
       and the noun itself. Thus, the masculine noun
       <i>
        li veisins
       </i>
       "the neighbour" (Latin
       <i>
        vicīnus
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /wɪˈkiːnʊs/
       </span>
       &gt; Proto-Romance *
       <i>
        vecínos
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /veˈt͡sinos/
       </span>
       &gt; OF
       <i>
        veisins
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /vejˈzĩns/
       </span>
       ; Modern French
       <i>
        le voisin
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /vwazɛ̃/
       </span>
       ) was declined as follows:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <caption>
        Evolution of the nominal masculine inflection from Classical Latin to Old French
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2">
        </th>
        <th>
         Latin
        </th>
        <th>
         Vulgar Latin
        </th>
        <th>
         Old French
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Singular
        </th>
        <th>
         nominative
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          ille vicīnus
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          (il)le vicīnos
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          li veisins
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         oblique (Latin accusative)
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          illum vicīnum
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          (il)lo vicīno
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          le veisin
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         Plural
        </th>
        <th>
         nominative
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          illī vicīnī
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          (il)lī vicīni
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          li veisin
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         oblique (Latin accusative)
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          illōs vicīnōs
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          (il)los vicīnos
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les veisins
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In later Old French, the distinctions had become moribund. As in most other Romance languages, it was the
       <a href="/wiki/Oblique_case" title="Oblique case">
        oblique case
       </a>
       form that usually survived to become the Modern French form:
       <i>
        l'enfant
       </i>
       "the child" represents the old oblique (Latin accusative
       <i>
        īnf
        <b>
         ā
        </b>
        ntem
       </i>
       ); the Old French nominative was
       <i>
        li enfes
       </i>
       (Latin
       <i>
        <b>
         ī
        </b>
        nfāns
       </i>
       ). There are some cases with significant differences between nominative and oblique forms (derived from Latin nouns with a stress shift between the nominative and other cases) in which either it is the nominative form that survives or both forms survive with different meanings:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Both OFr
        <i>
         li sire
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         le sieur
        </i>
        (Latin
        <i>
         s
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         iior
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         seii
         <b>
          ō
         </b>
         rem
        </i>
        ) and
        <i>
         le seignor
        </i>
        (nom.
        <sup>
         †
        </sup>
        <i>
         sendra
        </i>
        ;
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-24">
          [24]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Latin
        <i>
         s
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         nior
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         seni
         <b>
          ō
         </b>
         rem
        </i>
        ) survive in the vocabulary of later French (
        <i>
         messire
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         le sieur
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         seigneur
        </i>
        ) as different ways to refer to a feudal
        <a href="/wiki/Lord" title="Lord">
         lord
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Modern French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/s%C5%93ur" title="wikt:sœur">
          sœur
         </a>
        </i>
        "sister" is the nominative form (Old French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/suer" title="wikt:suer">
          suer
         </a>
        </i>
        &lt; Latin nominative
        <i>
         s
         <b>
          o
         </b>
         ror
        </i>
        ); the Old French oblique form
        <i>
         seror
        </i>
        (&lt; Latin accusative
        <i>
         sor
         <b>
          ō
         </b>
         rem
        </i>
        ) no longer survives.
       </li>
       <li>
        Modern French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pr%C3%AAtre" title="wikt:prêtre">
          prêtre
         </a>
        </i>
        "priest" is the nominative form (Old French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/prestre" title="wikt:prestre">
          prestre
         </a>
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         pr
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         sbyter
        </i>
        ); the Old French oblique form
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/prevoire" title="wikt:prevoire">
          prevoire
         </a>
        </i>
        , later
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/provoire" title="wikt:provoire">
          provoire
         </a>
        </i>
        (&lt;
        <i>
         presb
         <b>
          y
         </b>
         terem
        </i>
        ) survives only in the Paris street name
        <i>
         Rue des Prouvaires
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Modern French indefinite pronoun
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/on" title="wikt:on">
          on
         </a>
        </i>
        "one" continues Old French nominative
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hom" title="wikt:hom">
          hom
         </a>
        </i>
        "man" (&lt;
        <i>
         h
         <b>
          o
         </b>
         mō
        </i>
        );
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/homme" title="wikt:homme">
          homme
         </a>
        </i>
        "man" continues the oblique form (OF
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/home" title="wikt:home">
          home
         </a>
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         h
         <b>
          o
         </b>
         minem
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In a few cases in which the only distinction between forms was the nominative
       <i>
        -s
       </i>
       ending, the
       <i>
        -s
       </i>
       was preserved in spelling to distinguish otherwise-homonymous words. An example is
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fils" title="wikt:fils">
         fils
        </a>
       </i>
       "son" (&lt; Latin nominative
       <i>
        f
        <b>
         ī
        </b>
        lius
       </i>
       ), spelled to distinguish it from
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fil" title="wikt:fil">
         fil
        </a>
       </i>
       "wire". In this case, a later
       <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">
        spelling pronunciation
       </a>
       has resulted in the modern pronunciation
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /fis/
       </span>
       (earlier
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /fi/
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       As in Spanish and Italian, the neuter gender was eliminated, and most old neuter nouns became masculine. Some Latin neuter plurals were reanalysed as feminine singulars: Latin
       <i>
        gaudium
       </i>
       was more widely used in the plural form
       <i>
        gaudia
       </i>
       , which was taken for a singular in Vulgar Latin and ultimately led to modern French
       <i>
        la joie
       </i>
       , "joy" (feminine singular).
      </p>
      <p>
       Nouns were declined in the following
       <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">
        declensions
       </a>
       :
      </p>

        </td>

         </i>

        <th colspan="2">

        </th>
        <th>
         "singer"
        </th>
        <th>
         "baron"
        </th>
        <th>
         "nun"
        </th>
        <th>
         "sister"
        </th>
        <th>
         "child"
        </th>
        <th>
         "priest"
        </th>
        <th>
         "lord"
        </th>
        <th>
         "count"
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th rowspan="2">
         sg.
        </th>
        <th>
         <small>
          nominative
         </small>
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          li chantere
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>

          li ber
         </i>
        </td>

          la none
         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

          li enfes
         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <small>
          oblique
         </small>
        </th>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

         </i>
        </td>

          le prevoire
         </i>
        </td>

         </i>

         </i>
        </td>

        </th>

         </small>

         </i>

         </i>

         </i>

         </i>

         </i>

        <th>
         <small>
          oblique
         </small>
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          les chanteors
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les barons
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les nonains
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les serors
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les enfanz
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les prevoires
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les sieurs
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          les contes
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Class I is derived from the Latin
       <a href="/wiki/First_declension" title="First declension">
        first declension
       </a>
       . Class Ia mostly comes from Latin feminine nouns in the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_declension" title="Third declension">
        third declension
       </a>
       . Class II is derived from the Latin
       <a href="/wiki/Second_declension" title="Second declension">
        second declension
       </a>
       . Class IIa generally stems from second-declension nouns ending in
       <i>
        -er
       </i>
       and from third-declension masculine nouns; in both cases, the Latin nominative singular did not end in
       <i>
        -s
       </i>
       , which is preserved in Old French.
      </p>
      <p>
       The classes show various analogical developments:
       <i>
        -es
       </i>
       from the accusative instead of
       <i>
        -∅
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       after a consonant cluster) in Class I nominative plural (Latin
       <i>
        -ae
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        li pere
       </i>
       instead of
       <i>
        *li peres
       </i>
       (Latin
       <i>
        illi patres
       </i>
       ) in Class IIa nominative plural, modelled on Class II, etc.
      </p>
      <p>
       Class III nouns show a separate form in the nominative singular that does not occur in any of the other forms. IIIa nouns ended in
       <i>
        -
        <b>
         ā
        </b>
        tor
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        -āt
        <b>
         ō
        </b>
        rem
       </i>
       in Latin and preserve the stress shift; IIIb nouns also had a stress shift, from
       <i>
        -ō
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        -
        <b>
         ō
        </b>
        nem
       </i>
       . IIIc nouns are an Old French creation and have no clear Latin antecedent. IIId nouns represent various other types of third-declension Latin nouns with stress shift or a change of consonant (
       <i>
        s
        <b>
         o
        </b>
        ror
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sor
        <b>
         ō
        </b>
        rem;
        <b>
         ī
        </b>
        nfāns, īnf
        <b>
         ā
        </b>
        ntem; pr
        <b>
         e
        </b>
        sbyter, presb
        <b>
         y
        </b>
        terem; s
        <b>
         e
        </b>
        iior, seii
        <b>
         ō
        </b>
        rem; c
        <b>
         o
        </b>
        mes, c
        <b>
         o
        </b>
        mitem).
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       Regular feminine forms of masculine nouns are formed by adding an
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       to the masculine stem unless the masculine stem already ends in
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       . For example,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bergier" title="wikt:bergier">
         bergier
        </a>
       </i>
       (shepherd) becomes
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bergiere" title="wikt:bergiere">
         bergiere
        </a>
       </i>
       (Modern French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/berger" title="wikt:berger">
         berger
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/berg%C3%A8re" title="wikt:bergère">
         bergère
        </a>
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">
        Adjectives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Adjectives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Adjectives agree in terms of
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">
        number
       </a>
       , gender and case with the noun that they are qualifying. Thus, a feminine plural noun in the nominative case requires any qualifying adjectives to be feminine, plural and nominative. For example, in
       <i>
        femes riches
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/riche#Old_French" title="wikt:riche">
         riche
        </a>
       </i>
       has to be in the feminine plural form.
      </p>
      <p>
       Adjectives can be divided into three declensional classes:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Class I corresponding roughly to
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension#First_and_second_declension_adjectives" title="Latin declension">
         Latin 1st- and 2nd-declension adjectives
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Class II corresponding roughly to
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension#Third_declension_adjectives" title="Latin declension">
         Latin 3rd-declension adjectives
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Class III containing primarily the descendants of
        <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension#Comparative_and_superlative_forms_of_adjectives" title="Latin declension">
         Latin synthetic comparative forms
        </a>
        in
        <i>
         -ior
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         -iōrem
        </i>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Class I adjectives have a feminine singular form (nominative and oblique) ending in
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       . They can be further subdivided into two subclasses, based on the masculine nominative singular form. Class Ia adjectives have a masculine nominative singular ending in
       <i>
        -s
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bon#Old_French" title="wikt:bon">
          bon
         </a>
        </i>
        "good" (&lt; Latin
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bonus#Latin" title="wikt:bonus">
          bonus
         </a>
        </i>
        , &gt; modern French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bon#French" title="wikt:bon">
          bon
         </a>
        </i>
        )
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>
          <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
           Masculine
          </th>
          <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
           Feminine
          </th>
          <th>
           Neuter
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th>

          </th>

          </th>
         </tr>

          </td>

          </td>

           </i>
          </td>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th>
           Oblique
          </th>
          <td>
           <i>
            bon
           </i>
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>
            bons
           </i>
          </td>
          <td>
           —
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       For Class Ib adjectives, the masculine nominative singular ends in
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       , like the feminine. There are descendants of Latin second- and third-declension adjectives ending in
       <i>
        -er
       </i>
       in the nominative singular:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         aspre
        </i>
        "harsh" (&lt; Latin
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/asper#Latin" title="wikt:asper">
          asper
         </a>
        </i>
        , &gt; modern French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C3%A2pre#French" title="wikt:âpre">
          âpre
         </a>
        </i>
        )
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>
          <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
           Masculine
          </th>

           Feminine
          </th>
          <th>
           Neuter
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>

          </th>

          </th>
         </tr>

          </th>

           <i>

           </i>
          </td>
          <td>
           <i>

          </td>

          <td rowspan="2">

          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       For Class II adjectives, the feminine singular is not marked by the ending
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/granz#Old_French" title="wikt:granz">
          granz
         </a>
        </i>
        "big, great" (&lt; Latin
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/grandis#Latin" title="wikt:grandis">
          grandis
         </a>
        </i>
        , &gt; modern French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/grand#French" title="wikt:grand">
          grand
         </a>
        </i>
        )
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>
          <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
           Masculine
          </th>
          <th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">
           Feminine
          </th>
          <th>
           Neuter
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>
          <th>
           Singular
          </th>
          <th>
           Plural
          </th>
          <th>

          </th>
          <th>

          </th>
          <th>

          </th>
         </tr>

          </th>

           <i>

           <i>

           </i>

           </i>
          </td>

          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       An important subgroup of Class II adjectives is the present participial forms in
       <i>
        -ant
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Class III adjectives have a stem alternation, resulting from stress shift in the Latin third declension and a distinct neuter form:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/meillor#Old_French" title="wikt:meillor">
          mieudre
         </a>
        </i>
        "better" (&lt; Latin
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/melior#Latin" title="wikt:melior">
          melior
         </a>
        </i>
        , &gt; modern French
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/meilleur#French" title="wikt:meilleur">
          meilleur
         </a>
        </i>
        )
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <table class="wikitable">
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>

          </th>

          </th>
          <th>
           Neuter
          </th>
         </tr>
         <tr>
          <th>
          </th>

           Singular
          </th>

           </i>

           </i>
          </td>

          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In later Old French, Classes II and III tended to be moved across to Class I, which was complete by Middle French. Modern French thus has only a single adjective declension, unlike most other Romance languages, which have two or more.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">
        Verbs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Verbs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Verbs in Old French show the same extreme phonological deformations as other Old French words. Morphologically, however, Old French verbs are extremely conservative in preserving intact most of the Latin alternations and irregularities that had been inherited in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Romance" title="Proto-Romance">
        Proto-Romance
       </a>
       . Old French has much less analogical reformation than Modern French has and significantly less than the oldest stages of other languages (such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Spanish" title="Old Spanish">
        Old Spanish
       </a>
       ) despite the fact that the various
       <a href="/wiki/Phonological_development" title="Phonological development">
        phonological developments
       </a>
       in Gallo-Romance and Proto-French led to complex alternations in the majority of commonly-used verbs.
      </p>
      <p>
       For example, the Old French verb
       <i>
        laver
       </i>
       "to wash" (Latin
       <i>
        lav
        <b>
         ā
        </b>
        re
       </i>
       ) is conjugated
       <i>
        je lef
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tu leves
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        il leve
       </i>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Present_indicative" title="Present indicative">
        present indicative
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        je lef
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tu les
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        il let
       </i>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Present_subjunctive" title="Present subjunctive">
        present subjunctive
       </a>
       , in both cases regular phonological developments from Latin indicative
       <i>
        l
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        vō
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        l
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        vās
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        l
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        vat
       </i>
       and subjunctive
       <i>
        l
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        vem
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        l
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        vēs
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        l
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        vet
       </i>
       . The following paradigm is typical in showing the phonologically regular but morphologically irregular alternations of most paradigms:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The alternation
        <i>
         je lef
        </i>
        ~
        <i>
         tu leves
        </i>
        is a regular result of the final
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devoicing" title="Devoicing">
         devoicing
        </a>
        triggered by loss of final /o/ but not /a/.
       </li>
       <li>
        The alternation
        <i>
         laver
        </i>
        ~
        <i>
         tu leves
        </i>
        is a regular result of the diphthongization of a stressed
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_syllable" title="Open syllable">
         open syllable
        </a>
        /a/ into /ae/ &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /æ/
        </span>
        &gt;
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /e/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        The alternation
        <i>
         je lef
        </i>
        ~
        <i>
         tu les
        </i>
        ~
        <i>
         il let
        </i>
        in the subjunctive is a regular result of the simplification of the final clusters /fs/ and /ft/, resulting from loss of /e/ in final syllables.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Modern French, on the other hand, has
       <i>
        je lave
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tu laves
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        il lave
       </i>
       in both indicative and subjunctive, reflecting significant analogical developments: analogical borrowing of unstressed vowel /a/, analogical
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       in the first singular (from verbs like
       <i>
        j'entre
       </i>
       , with a regular
       <i>
        -e
       </i>
       ) and wholesale replacement of the subjunctive with forms modelled on
       <i>
        -ir
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        -oir
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        -re
       </i>
       verbs. All serve to eliminate the various alternations in the Old French verb paradigm. Even modern "irregular" verbs are not immune from analogy: For example, Old French
       <i>
        je vif
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tu vis
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        il vit
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        vivre
       </i>
       "to live") has yielded to modern
       <i>
        je vis
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tu vis
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        il vit
       </i>
       , eliminating the unpredictable
       <i>
        -f
       </i>
       in the first-person singular.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Simple_past" title="Simple past">
        simple past
       </a>
       also shows extensive analogical reformation and simplification in Modern French, as compared with Old French.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Latin
       <a href="/wiki/Pluperfect" title="Pluperfect">
        pluperfect
       </a>
       was preserved in very early Old French as a past tense with a value similar to a
       <a href="/wiki/Preterite" title="Preterite">
        preterite
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Imperfect" title="Imperfect">
        imperfect
       </a>
       . For example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sequence_of_Saint_Eulalia" title="Sequence of Saint Eulalia">
        Sequence of Saint Eulalia
       </a>
       (878 AD) has past-tense forms such as
       <i>
        avret
       </i>
       (&lt; Latin
       <i>
        habuerat
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        voldret
       </i>
       (&lt; Latin
       <i>
        voluerat
       </i>
       ), alternating with past-tense forms from the Latin perfect (continued as the modern "simple past").
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Occitan" title="Old Occitan">
        Old Occitan
       </a>
       also preserved this tense, with a
       <a href="/wiki/Conditional_mood" title="Conditional mood">
        conditional
       </a>
       value;
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       still preserves this tense (the
       <i>
        -ra
       </i>
       imperfect subjunctive), as does
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       (in its original value as a pluperfect indicative).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Verb_alternations">
        Verb alternations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Verb alternations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In Latin, stress was determined automatically by the number of syllables in a word and the
       <a href="/wiki/Syllable_weight" title="Syllable weight">
        weight
       </a>
       (length) of the syllables. That resulted in certain automatic stress shifts between related forms in a paradigm, depending on the nature of the suffixes added. For example, in
       <i>
        p
        <b>
         e
        </b>
        nsō
       </i>
       "I think", the first syllable was stressed, but in
       <i>
        pens
        <b>
         ā
        </b>
        mus
       </i>
       "we think", the second syllable was stressed. In many Romance languages, vowels diphthongized in stressed syllables under certain circumstances but not in unstressed syllables, resulting in alternations in verb paradigms:
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       <i>
        pienso
       </i>
       "I think" vs.
       <i>
        pensamos
       </i>
       "we think" (
       <i>
        pensar
       </i>
       "to think"), or
       <i>
        cuento
       </i>
       "I tell" vs.
       <i>
        contamos
       </i>
       "we tell" (
       <i>
        contar
       </i>
       "to tell").
      </p>
      <p>
       In the development of French, at least five vowels diphthongized in stressed,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_syllable" title="Open syllable">
        open syllables
       </a>
       . Combined with other stress-dependent developments, that yielded 15 or so types of alternations in so-called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_verb" title="Irregular verb">
        strong verbs
       </a>
       in Old French. For example,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       diphthongized to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ai/
       </span>
       before
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" title="Nasal stop">
        nasal stops
       </a>
       in stressed, open syllables but not in unstressed syllables, yielding
       <i>
        aim
       </i>
       "I love" (Latin
       <i>
        <b>
         a
        </b>
        mō
       </i>
       ) but
       <i>
        amons
       </i>
       "we love" (Latin
       <i>
        am
        <b>
         ā
        </b>
        mus
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       The different types are as follows:
      </p>

         <i>
          dēmōrārī
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          demeure
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          demo(u)rer
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "to stay"
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          cōnsuere
         </i>
         &gt;
         <br/>
         <i>
          *cōsere
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          queust
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          cousdre
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "to sew"
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /u/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /e/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         intertonic blocked
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /o/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          *corruptiāre
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          courouce
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          courecier
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "to get angry"
        </td>
        <td colspan="4">
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ũ/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ã/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         intertonic blocked
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /o/
         </span>
         + nasal
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          calumniārī
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          chalonge
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          chalengier
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         "to challenge"
        </td>
        <td colspan="4">
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In Modern French, the verbs in the
       <i>
        -er
       </i>
       class have been systematically levelled. Generally, the "weak" (unstressed) form predominates, but there are some exceptions (such as modern
       <i>
        aimer
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        nous aimons
       </i>
       ). The only remaining alternations are in verbs like
       <i>
        acheter
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        j'achète
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        jeter
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        je jette
       </i>
       , with unstressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ə/
       </span>
       alternating with stressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       and in (largely-learned) verbs like
       <i>
        adhérer/j'adhère
       </i>
       , with unstressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       alternating with stressed
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɛ/
       </span>
       . Many of the non-
       <i>
        er
       </i>
       verbs have become obsolete, and many of the remaining verbs have been levelled. A few alternations remain, however, in what are now known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_verbs" title="Irregular verbs">
        irregular verbs
       </a>
       , such as
       <i>
        je tiens
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        nous tenons
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        je dois
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        nous devons
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        je meurs
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        nous mourons
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Some verbs had a more irregular alternation between different-length stems, with a longer, stressed stem alternating with a shorter, unstressed stem. That was a regular development stemming from the loss of unstressed
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages#Intertonic_vowels" title="Romance languages">
        intertonic vowels
       </a>
       , which remained when they were stressed:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         j'aiu
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         aidier
        </i>
        "help" &lt;
        <i>
         adi
         <b>
          ū
         </b>
         tō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         adiūt
         <b>
          ā
         </b>
         re
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         j'araison
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         araisnier
        </i>
        "speak to" &lt;
        <i>
         adrati
         <b>
          ō
         </b>
         nō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         adratiōn
         <b>
          ā
         </b>
         re
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         je deraison
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         deraisnier
        </i>
        "argue" &lt;
        <i>
         dērati
         <b>
          ō
         </b>
         nō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         dēratiōn
         <b>
          ā
         </b>
         re
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         je desjun
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         disner
        </i>
        "dine" &lt;
        <i>
         disiēi
         <b>
          ū
         </b>
         nō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         disiēiūn
         <b>
          ā
         </b>
         re
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         je manju
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         mangier
        </i>
        "eat" &lt;
        <i>
         mand
         <b>
          ū
         </b>
         cō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mandūc
         <b>
          ā
         </b>
         re
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         je parol
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         parler
        </i>
        "speak" &lt;
        <i>
         *par
         <b>
          au
         </b>
         lō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         *paraul
         <b>
          ā
         </b>
         re
        </i>
        &lt;
        <i>
         parabolō
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         parabolāre
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The alternation of
       <i>
        je desjun
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        disner
       </i>
       is particularly complicated; it appears that
       <i>
        disiēiūnāre
       </i>
       &gt;
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Romance" title="Western Romance">
        Western Romance
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /desjejuˈnare
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /desjejˈnare/
       </span>
       (preliminary intertonic loss) &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /desiˈnare/
       </span>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Triphthong" title="Triphthong">
        triphthong
       </a>
       reduction) &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /disiˈnare/
       </span>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphony_(Romance_languages)" title="Metaphony (Romance languages)">
        metaphony
       </a>
       ) &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /disˈner/
       </span>
       (further intertonic loss and other proto-French developments). Both stems have become full verbs in Modern French:
       <i>
        déjeuner
       </i>
       "to have lunch" and
       <i>
        dîner
       </i>
       "to dine". Furthermore,
       <i>
        déjeuner
       </i>
       does not derive directly from
       <i>
        je desjun
       </i>
       (&lt;
       <i>
        *disi(ēi)ūnō
       </i>
       , with total loss of unstressed
       <i>
        -ēi-
       </i>
       ). Instead, it comes from Old French
       <i>
        desjeüner
       </i>
       , based on the alternative form
       <i>
        je desjeün
       </i>
       (&lt;
       <i>
        *disiē(i)ūnō
       </i>
       , with loss of only
       <i>
        -i-
       </i>
       , likely influenced by
       <i>
        jeûner
       </i>
       "to fast" &lt; Old French
       <i>
        jeüner
       </i>
       &lt;
       <i>
        je jeün
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d͡ʒe.ˈyn/
       </span>
       "I fast" &lt;
       <i>
        iē(i)ūnō
       </i>
       :
       <i>
        iē-
       </i>
       is an initial rather than intertonic so the vowel
       <i>
        -ē-
       </i>
       does not disappear).
      </p>

         <i>
          (i)ert

      <p>
       Non-finite forms:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Infinitive:
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/estre" title="wikt:estre">
         <i>
          estre
         </i>
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Present participle:
        <i>
         estant
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        Past participle:
        <i>
         esté(t)
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Auxiliary verb:
       <i>
        avoir
       </i>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_parts_of_speech">
        Other parts of speech
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Old_French&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Other parts of speech">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Adverbs, prepositions, conjunctions and interjections are generally invariable, one notable exception being the adverb
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tot#Old_French" title="wikt:tot">
         tot
        </a>
       </i>
       , like Modern French
       <i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tout#French" title="wikt:tout">
         tout
        </a>
       </i>
       : all, every.
      </p>

           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Etymology+of+%27%26%2339%3Bframbuesa%27%26%2339%3B+%28Spanish%29&amp;rft.pub=Buscon.rae.es&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fbuscon.rae.es%2FdraeI%2FSrvltConsulta%3FTIPO_BUS%3D3%26LEMA%3Dframbuesa&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOld+French">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-17">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-17">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Portuguese
           <i>
            framboesa
           </i>
           ‘raspberry’ and Spanish
           <i>
            frambuesa
           </i>
           are French loans.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-18">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-18">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <i>
            La Chanson de Roland.
           </i>
           Edited and Translated into Modern French by Ian Short. Paris: Livre de Poche, 1990. p. 12.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-2-253-05341-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-2-253-05341-5">
            978-2-253-05341-5
           </a>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-19">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-19">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <span class="languageicon">
            (in French)
           </span>
           Antoine Adam, Georges Lerminier, and Édouard Morot-Sir, eds.
           <i>
            Littérature française.
           </i>
           "Tome 1: Des origines à la fin du XVIIIe siècle," Paris: Larousse, 1967, p. 16.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-20">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-20">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <span class="languageicon">
            (in French)
           </span>
           Antoine Adam, Georges Lerminier, and Édouard Morot-Sir, eds.
           <i>
            Littérature française.
           </i>
           "Tome 1: Des origines à la fin du XVIIIe siècle," Paris: Larousse, 1967, p. 36-37.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-21">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-21">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The chart is based on phonologies given in Laborderie, Noëlle,
           <i>
            Précis de Phonétique Historique
           </i>
           , Nathan 1994; and in Rickard, Peter,
           <i>
            A History of the French Language
           </i>
           , 2nd edition, Routledge 1989, pp. 47-8.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-22">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-22">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           <cite class="citation book">
            Berthon, H. E.; Starkey, V. G. (1908).
            <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/tablessynoptique00bert#page/12/mode/1up" rel="nofollow">
             <i>
              Tables synoptiques de phonologie de l'ancien français
             </i>
            </a>
            . Oxford Clarendon Press.
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Tables+synoptiques+de+phonologie+de+l%27ancien+fran%C3%A7ais&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+Clarendon+Press&amp;rft.date=1908&amp;rft.au=Berthon%2C+H.+E.&amp;rft.au=Starkey%2C+V.+G.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fstream%2Ftablessynoptique00bert%23page%2F12%2Fmode%2F1up&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AOld+French">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-23">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-23">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Zink (1999), p. 132
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-24">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-24">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The Old French nominative
           <i>
            sendra
           </i>
           , inherited from Latin
           <i>
            senior
           </i>
           , appears only in the
           <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">
            Oaths of Strasbourg
           </a>
           before it become obsolete.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-25">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-25">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Moignet (1988, p. 26–31), Zink (1992, p. 39–48), de La Chaussée (1977, p. 39–44)
          </span>

        <a class="external text" href="http://www.reocities.com/Athens/Acropolis/8716/" rel="nofollow">
         Old French on the Web
        </a>

         Old French Online

        from the University of Texas at Austin
       </li>

        <a class="external text" href="http://www.lexilogos.com/francais_dictionnaire_ancien.htm" rel="nofollow">
         Lexilogos: Online dictionaries of Old French
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.atilf.fr/dect" rel="nofollow">
         DÉCT
        </a>
        - (Electronic Dictionary of Chretien de Troyes) : complete lexicon and transcriptions of the five romances of this Old French author. University of Ottawa - CNRS.
       </li>

        <cite class="citation book">
         Du Bellay, Joachim (1549).
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.wdl.org/en/item/4357/" rel="nofollow">
          <i>

           <div class="hlist hlist-separated">
            <ul>
             <li>
              <i>
               Italics
              </i>
              indicate
              <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">
               extinct languages
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              <b>
               Bold
              </b>
              indicates
              <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">
               languages with more than 5 million speakers
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              Languages between parentheses are
              <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
               varieties
              </a>
              of the language on their left.
             </li>
